<p>A <b>child</b> (<a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural"><span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">PL</span></span></a>: <b>children</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human</a> being between the stages of <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">birth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Child_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Child-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosby_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mosby-2">[2]</a></sup> or between the <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_human_body" title="Development of the human body">developmental period</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infancy" title="Infancy">infancy</a> and puberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rathus_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rathus-3">[3]</a></sup> The legal definition of <i>child</i> generally refers to a <a href="/wiki/Minor_(law)" title="Minor (law)">minor</a>, otherwise known as a person younger than the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_majority" title="Age of majority">age of majority</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Child_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Child-1">[1]</a></sup> Children generally have fewer <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_rights" title="Children's rights">rights</a> and responsibilities than <a href="/wiki/Adult" title="Adult">adults</a>. They are classed as unable to make serious decisions. 
</p><p><i>Child</i> may also describe a relationship with a parent (such as <a href="/wiki/Son" title="Son">sons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daughter" title="Daughter">daughters</a> of any age)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> or, metaphorically, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authority_figure" title="Authority figure">authority figure</a>, or signify group membership in a clan, tribe, or religion; it can also signify being strongly affected by a specific time, place, or circumstance, as in "a child of nature" or "a child of the Sixties.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>

</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Developmental_stages_post-childhood"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Developmental stages post-childhood</span></a>

<h2><span id="Biological.2C_legal_and_social_definitions"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological,_legal_and_social_definitions">Biological, legal and social definitions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Children_games_Louvre_Ma99_n2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2120" data-file-width="3775" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Children_games_Louvre_Ma99_n2.jpg/220px-Children_games_Louvre_Ma99_n2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Children_games_Louvre_Ma99_n2.jpg/330px-Children_games_Louvre_Ma99_n2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Children_games_Louvre_Ma99_n2.jpg/440px-Children_games_Louvre_Ma99_n2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Children_games_Louvre_Ma99_n2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children playing ball games, Roman artwork, 2nd century AD</div></div></div>
<p>In the biological sciences, a child is usually defined as a person between birth and puberty,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Child_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Child-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosby_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mosby-2">[2]</a></sup> or between the <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_human_body" title="Development of the human body">developmental period</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infancy" title="Infancy">infancy</a> and puberty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rathus_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rathus-3">[3]</a></sup> Legally, the term <i>child</i> may refer to anyone below the age of majority or some other age limit.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Rights_of_the_Child" title="Convention on the Rights of the Child">Convention on the Rights of the Child</a> defines <i>child</i> as, "A human being below the age of 18 years unless under the <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a> applicable to the child, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_majority" title="Age of majority">majority</a> is attained earlier.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-un_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-un-6">[6]</a></sup> This is ratified by 192 of 194 member countries. The term <i>child</i> may also refer to someone below another legally defined age limit unconnected to the age of majority. In <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, for example, a <i>child</i> is legally defined as someone under the age of 14 under the "Children and Young Persons Act" whereas the age of majority is 21.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In U.S. Immigration Law, a child refers to anyone who is under the age of 21.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some English definitions of the word <i>child</i> include the <a href="/wiki/Fetus" title="Fetus">fetus</a> (sometimes termed <i>the unborn</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SeeShorter_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-SeeShorter-10">[10]</a></sup> In many cultures, a child is considered an adult after undergoing a <a href="/wiki/Rite_of_passage" title="Rite of passage">rite of passage</a>, which may or may not correspond to the time of puberty.
</p><p>Children generally have fewer rights than adults and are classed as unable to make serious decisions, and legally must always be under the care of a responsible adult or <a href="/wiki/Child_custody" title="Child custody">child custody</a>, whether their parents divorce or not. 
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Developmental_stages_of_childhood">Developmental stages of childhood</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Child_development_stages" title="Child development stages">Child development stages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_development" title="Child development">Child development</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_childhood">Early childhood</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Children_Playing_Violin_Suzuki_Institute_2011.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1305" data-file-width="1707" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Children_Playing_Violin_Suzuki_Institute_2011.JPG/220px-Children_Playing_Violin_Suzuki_Institute_2011.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Children_Playing_Violin_Suzuki_Institute_2011.JPG/330px-Children_Playing_Violin_Suzuki_Institute_2011.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Children_Playing_Violin_Suzuki_Institute_2011.JPG/440px-Children_Playing_Violin_Suzuki_Institute_2011.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Children_Playing_Violin_Suzuki_Institute_2011.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children playing the <a href="/wiki/Violin" title="Violin">violin</a> in a group recital, Ithaca, New York, 2011</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malagasy_girls_Madagascar_Merina.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Malagasy_girls_Madagascar_Merina.jpg/220px-Malagasy_girls_Madagascar_Merina.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Malagasy_girls_Madagascar_Merina.jpg/330px-Malagasy_girls_Madagascar_Merina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Malagasy_girls_Madagascar_Merina.jpg/440px-Malagasy_girls_Madagascar_Merina.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malagasy_girls_Madagascar_Merina.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, 2011</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Child_playing_piano_-_1984-11-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4123" data-file-width="5639" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Child_playing_piano_-_1984-11-01.jpg/220px-Child_playing_piano_-_1984-11-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Child_playing_piano_-_1984-11-01.jpg/330px-Child_playing_piano_-_1984-11-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Child_playing_piano_-_1984-11-01.jpg/440px-Child_playing_piano_-_1984-11-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Child_playing_piano_-_1984-11-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Child playing piano, 1984</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Early_childhood" title="Early childhood">Early childhood</a> follows the <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infancy</a> stage and begins with <a href="/wiki/Toddler" title="Toddler">toddlerhood</a> when the child begins speaking or taking steps independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> While toddlerhood ends around age 3 when the child becomes less dependent on parental assistance for basic needs, early childhood continues approximately until the age of 6 or 7. However, according to the <a href="/wiki/National_Association_for_the_Education_of_Young_Children" title="National Association for the Education of Young Children">National Association for the Education of Young Children</a>, early childhood also includes infancy. At this stage children are learning through observing, experimenting and communicating with others. Adults supervise and support the development process of the child, which then will lead to the child's autonomy. Also during this stage, a strong emotional bond is created between the child and the care providers. The children also start preschool and kindergarten at this age: and hence their social lives.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_childhood">Middle childhood</span></h3>
<p>Middle childhood begins at around age 7, approximating primary school age. It ends with puberty (around age 12 or 13), which typically marks the beginning of adolescence. In this period, children develop socially and mentally. They are at a stage where they make new friends and gain new skills, which will enable them to become more independent and enhance their individuality.
During middle childhood, children enter the school years, where they are presented with a different setting than they are used to. This new setting creates new challenges and faces for children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_1984_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins_1984-13">[13]</a></sup> Upon the entrance of school, mental disorders that would normally not be noticed come to light. Many of these disorders include: autism, dyslexia, dyscalculia, and ADHD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger_2017_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berger_2017-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 303–309">: 303–309 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Special_education" title="Special education">Special education</a>, <a href="/wiki/Least_restrictive_environment" title="Least restrictive environment">least restrictive environment</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Response_to_intervention" title="Response to intervention">response to intervention</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Individualized_education_plan" title="Individualized education plan">individualized education plans</a> are all specialized plans to help children with disabilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger_2017_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Berger_2017-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 310–311">: 310–311 </span></sup>
Middle childhood is the time when children begin to understand responsibility and are beginning to be shaped by their peers and parents. Chores and more responsible decisions come at this time, and so does social comparison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger_2017_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Berger_2017-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 338">: 338 </span></sup> Along with social comparison comes social play. With social play comes learning and teaching. During social play, children learn from and teach each other, often through observation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Developmental_stages_post-childhood">Developmental stages post-childhood</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adolescence">Adolescence</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Adolescence" title="Adolescence">Adolescence</a> is usually determined to be between the onset of puberty and legal adulthood: mostly corresponding to the teenage years (13-19). However, <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a> usually begins before the teenage years. Although biologically a child is a human being between the stages of <a href="/wiki/Childbirth" title="Childbirth">birth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Puberty" title="Puberty">puberty</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Child_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Child-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosby_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mosby-2">[2]</a></sup> adolescents are legally children, as adolescents tend to lack adult rights and are still required to attend compulsory schooling in many cultures, though this varies. The onset of adolescence brings about various physical, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychological" title="Psychological">psychological</a> and behavioral changes. The end of adolescence and the beginning of adulthood varies by country and by function, and even within a single nation-state or culture there may be different ages at which an individual is considered to be mature enough to be entrusted by society with certain tasks. 
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_childhood" title="History of childhood">History of childhood</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Su_Han_Ch%27en_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2295" data-file-width="1256" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Su_Han_Ch%27en_001.jpg/170px-Su_Han_Ch%27en_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Su_Han_Ch%27en_001.jpg/255px-Su_Han_Ch%27en_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Su_Han_Ch%27en_001.jpg/340px-Su_Han_Ch%27en_001.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Su_Han_Ch%27en_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Playing Children</i>, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" title="Song Dynasty">Song Dynasty</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_art" title="Chinese art">Chinese artist</a> Su Hanchen, c. 1150 AD.</div></div></div>
<p>During the European <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, artistic depictions of children increased dramatically, which did not have much effect on the social attitude toward children, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 1600s, the concept of childhood began to emerge in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Adults saw children as separate beings, innocent and in need of protection and training by the adults around them. The English philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> was particularly influential in defining this new attitude towards children, especially with regard to his theory of the <a href="/wiki/Tabula_rasa" title="Tabula rasa">tabula rasa</a>, which considered the mind at birth to be a "blank slate". A corollary of this doctrine was that the mind of the child was born blank, and that it was the duty of the parents to imbue the child with correct notions. During the early period of <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a>, the rise of a large, commercial middle class, mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> countries of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">Dutch Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, brought about a new family ideology centred around the upbringing of children. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puritanism" title="Puritanism">Puritanism</a> stressed the importance of individual salvation and concern for the spiritual welfare of children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fig_1_Sir_Joshua_Reynolds_The_Age_of_Innocence._Painted_circa_1788._Frame_contemporary_with_picture._From_Houghton,_2005,_24.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="735" data-file-width="642" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Fig_1_Sir_Joshua_Reynolds_The_Age_of_Innocence._Painted_circa_1788._Frame_contemporary_with_picture._From_Houghton%2C_2005%2C_24.jpg/170px-Fig_1_Sir_Joshua_Reynolds_The_Age_of_Innocence._Painted_circa_1788._Frame_contemporary_with_picture._From_Houghton%2C_2005%2C_24.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Fig_1_Sir_Joshua_Reynolds_The_Age_of_Innocence._Painted_circa_1788._Frame_contemporary_with_picture._From_Houghton%2C_2005%2C_24.jpg/255px-Fig_1_Sir_Joshua_Reynolds_The_Age_of_Innocence._Painted_circa_1788._Frame_contemporary_with_picture._From_Houghton%2C_2005%2C_24.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Fig_1_Sir_Joshua_Reynolds_The_Age_of_Innocence._Painted_circa_1788._Frame_contemporary_with_picture._From_Houghton%2C_2005%2C_24.jpg/340px-Fig_1_Sir_Joshua_Reynolds_The_Age_of_Innocence._Painted_circa_1788._Frame_contemporary_with_picture._From_Houghton%2C_2005%2C_24.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fig_1_Sir_Joshua_Reynolds_The_Age_of_Innocence._Painted_circa_1788._Frame_contemporary_with_picture._From_Houghton,_2005,_24.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Innocence_(painting)" title="The Age of Innocence (painting)">The Age of Innocence</a></i> c. 1785/8. Reynolds emphasized the natural grace of children in his paintings</div></div></div>
<p>The modern notion of childhood with its own autonomy and goals began to emerge during the 18th century <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic period</a> that followed it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohen_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-cohen-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reeves_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-reeves-20">[20]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jean_Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean Jacques Rousseau">Jean Jacques Rousseau</a> formulated the romantic attitude towards children in his famous 1762 novel <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile:_or,_On_Education" title="Emile: or, On Education">Emile: or, On Education</a></i>. Building on the ideas of <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> and other 17th-century thinkers, Jean-Jaques Rousseau described childhood as a brief period of sanctuary before people encounter the perils and hardships of adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohen_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-cohen-19">[19]</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Joshua_Reynolds" title="Joshua Reynolds">Joshua Reynolds</a>' extensive children portraiture demonstrated the new enlightened attitudes toward young children. His 1788 painting <i><a href="/wiki/The_Age_of_Innocence_(painting)" title="The Age of Innocence (painting)">The Age of Innocence</a></i>, emphasizes the innocence and natural grace of the posing child and soon became a public favourite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-postle_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-postle-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The idea of childhood as a locus of divinity, purity, and innocence is further expounded upon in <a href="/wiki/William_Wordsworth" title="William Wordsworth">William Wordsworth</a>'s "Ode: Intimations of Immortality from Recollections of Early Childhood", the imagery of which he "fashioned from a complex mix of pastoral aesthetics, pantheistic views of divinity, and an idea of spiritual purity based on an Edenic notion of pastoral innocence infused with Neoplatonic notions of reincarnation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reeves_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-reeves-20">[20]</a></sup> This Romantic conception of childhood, historian Margaret Reeves suggests, has a longer history than generally recognized, with its roots traceable to similarly imaginative constructions of childhood circulating, for example, in the neo-platonic poetry of seventeenth-century metaphysical poet <a href="/wiki/Henry_Vaughan" title="Henry Vaughan">Henry Vaughan</a> (e.g., "The Retreate", 1650; "Childe-hood", 1655). Such views contrasted with the stridently didactic, Calvinist views of infant depravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReeves201841–42_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReeves201841–42-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vazken_Andr%C3%A9assian_HoMenEtMen-Sgaoudagan_doghantsk_Konstantinobolis_1918.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2605" data-file-width="1947" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Vazken_Andr%C3%A9assian_HoMenEtMen-Sgaoudagan_doghantsk_Konstantinobolis_1918.jpg/170px-Vazken_Andr%C3%A9assian_HoMenEtMen-Sgaoudagan_doghantsk_Konstantinobolis_1918.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Vazken_Andr%C3%A9assian_HoMenEtMen-Sgaoudagan_doghantsk_Konstantinobolis_1918.jpg/255px-Vazken_Andr%C3%A9assian_HoMenEtMen-Sgaoudagan_doghantsk_Konstantinobolis_1918.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Vazken_Andr%C3%A9assian_HoMenEtMen-Sgaoudagan_doghantsk_Konstantinobolis_1918.jpg/340px-Vazken_Andr%C3%A9assian_HoMenEtMen-Sgaoudagan_doghantsk_Konstantinobolis_1918.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vazken_Andr%C3%A9assian_HoMenEtMen-Sgaoudagan_doghantsk_Konstantinobolis_1918.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armenian scouts in 1918</div></div></div>
<p>With the onset of <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">industrialisation</a> in England in 1760, the divergence between high-minded romantic ideals of childhood and the reality of the growing magnitude of child exploitation in the workplace, became increasingly apparent. By the late 18th century, British children were specially employed in factories and mines and as <a href="/wiki/Chimney_sweep" title="Chimney sweep">chimney sweeps</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Del_Col_1930_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Del_Col_1930-23">[23]</a></sup> often working long hours in dangerous jobs for low pay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> As the century wore on, the contradiction between the conditions on the ground for poor children and the middle-class notion of childhood as a time of simplicity and innocence led to the first campaigns for the imposition of legal protection for children.
</p><p>British reformers attacked <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_labor" title="Child labor">child labor</a> from the 1830s onward, bolstered by the horrific descriptions of London street life by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The campaign eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/Factory_Acts" title="Factory Acts">Factory Acts</a>, which mitigated the exploitation of children at the workplace<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Del_Col_1930_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Del_Col_1930-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_concepts_of_childhood">Modern concepts of childhood</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fountain_Fun.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Fountain_Fun.jpg/220px-Fountain_Fun.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Fountain_Fun.jpg/330px-Fountain_Fun.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Fountain_Fun.jpg/440px-Fountain_Fun.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fountain_Fun.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children play in the fountain in a summer evening, <a href="/wiki/Davis,_California" title="Davis, California">Davis, California</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nepalese-children-with-cats.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Nepalese-children-with-cats.jpg/220px-Nepalese-children-with-cats.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Nepalese-children-with-cats.jpg/330px-Nepalese-children-with-cats.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Nepalese-children-with-cats.jpg/440px-Nepalese-children-with-cats.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nepalese-children-with-cats.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People_of_Nepal" title="People of Nepal">Nepalese</a> children playing with <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">cats</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Harari_Girls,_Ethiopia_(8261348010).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1038" data-file-width="886" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Harari_Girls%2C_Ethiopia_%288261348010%29.jpg/170px-Harari_Girls%2C_Ethiopia_%288261348010%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Harari_Girls%2C_Ethiopia_%288261348010%29.jpg/255px-Harari_Girls%2C_Ethiopia_%288261348010%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Harari_Girls%2C_Ethiopia_%288261348010%29.jpg/340px-Harari_Girls%2C_Ethiopia_%288261348010%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Harari_Girls,_Ethiopia_(8261348010).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Harari_people" title="Harari people">Harari</a> girls in Ethiopia.</div></div></div>
<p>The modern attitude to children emerged by the late 19th century; the Victorian middle and upper classes emphasized the role of the family and the sanctity of the child – an attitude that has remained dominant in Western societies ever since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The genre of <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_literature" title="Children's literature">children's literature</a> took off, with a proliferation of humorous, child-oriented books attuned to the child's imagination. <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll">Lewis Carroll</a>'s fantasy <i><a href="/wiki/Alice%27s_Adventures_in_Wonderland" title="Alice's Adventures in Wonderland">Alice's Adventures in Wonderland</a></i>, published in 1865 in England, was a landmark in the genre; regarded as the first "English masterpiece written for children", its publication opened the "First Golden Age" of children's literature.
</p><p>The latter half of the 19th century saw the introduction of compulsory state schooling of children across Europe, which decisively removed children from the workplace into schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
The market economy of the 19th century enabled the concept of childhood as a time of fun, happiness, and imagination. Factory-made dolls and doll houses delighted the girls and organized sports and activities were played by the boys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> 
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boy_Scout" title="Boy Scout">Boy Scouts</a> was founded by Sir <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_Baden-Powell" title="Robert Baden-Powell">Robert Baden-Powell</a> in 1908,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> which provided young boys with outdoor activities aiming at developing character, citizenship, and personal fitness qualities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Ari%C3%A8s" title="Philippe Ariès">Philippe Ariès</a>, a French historian specializing in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_history" title="Medieval history">medieval history</a>, suggested that childhood was not a natural phenomenon, but a creation of society in his 1960 book <i><a href="/wiki/Centuries_of_Childhood" title="Centuries of Childhood">Centuries of Childhood</a></i>. In 1961 he published a study of paintings, gravestones, furniture, and school records, finding that before the 17th-century, children were represented as mini-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adults" title="Adults">adults</a>.
</p><p>In 1966, the American philosopher <a href="/wiki/George_Boas" title="George Boas">George Boas</a> published the book <i>The Cult of Childhood</i>. Since then, historians have increasingly researched childhood in past times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006 <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Cunningham_(historian)" title="Hugh Cunningham (historian)">Hugh Cunningham</a>, published the book <i>Invention of Childhood</i> looking at British childhood from the year 1000, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> to what he refers to as the Post War Period of the 1950s, 1960s and 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Childhood evolves and changes as lifestyles change and adult expectations alter. In the modern era, many adults believe that children should not have any worries or work, as life should be happy and trouble-free. Childhood is seen as a mixture of simplicity, innocence, happiness, fun, imagination, and wonder. It is thought of as a time of playing, learning, socializing, exploring, and worrying in a world without much adult interference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohen_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-cohen-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reeves_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-reeves-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>A "loss of innocence" is a common concept, and is often seen as an integral part of <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_age" title="Coming of age">coming of age</a>. It is usually thought of as an experience or period in a child's life that widens their awareness of evil, pain or the world around them. This theme is demonstrated in the novels <i><a href="/wiki/To_Kill_a_Mockingbird" title="To Kill a Mockingbird">To Kill a Mockingbird</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Flies" title="Lord of the Flies">Lord of the Flies</a></i>. The fictional character <a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan" title="Peter Pan">Peter Pan</a> was the embodiment of a childhood that never ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Healthy_childhoods">Healthy childhoods</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_parents">Role of parents</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Parenting" title="Parenting">Parenting</a></div>
<h3><span id="Children.27s_health"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Children's_health">Children's health</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Childhood_obesity" title="Childhood obesity">Childhood obesity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Childhood_immunizations" title="Childhood immunizations">Childhood immunizations</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_childhood_diseases" title="List of childhood diseases">List of childhood diseases</a></div>
<p>Children's health includes the physical, mental and social well-being of children. Maintaining children's health implies offering them healthy foods, insuring they get enough sleep and exercise, and protecting their safety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Children in certain parts of the world often suffer from <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, which is often associated with other conditions, such diarrhea, pneumonia and malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_protection">Child protection</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_labor" title="Child labor">Child labor</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_labor_laws" title="Child labor laws">Child labor laws</a>, <a href="/wiki/Risk_aversion" title="Risk aversion">Risk aversion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">Child abuse</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Protection_of_Children_Act" title="Protection of Children Act">Protection of Children Act</a></div>
<p>Child protection, according to UNICEF, refers to "preventing and responding to violence, exploitation and abuse against children – including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commercial_sexual_exploitation" title="Commercial sexual exploitation">commercial sexual exploitation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_trafficking" title="Child trafficking">trafficking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">child labour</a> and harmful traditional practices, such as <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a>/cutting and <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">child marriage</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The
<a href="/wiki/Convention_on_the_Rights_of_the_Child" title="Convention on the Rights of the Child">Convention on the Rights of the Child</a> protects the fundamental rights of children.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Play">Play</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Play_(activity)" title="Play (activity)">Play (activity)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Playground" title="Playground">Playground</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_friend" title="Imaginary friend">Imaginary friend</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Childhood_secret_club" title="Childhood secret club">Childhood secret club</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MOPC_60.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="445" data-file-width="361" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/MOPC_60.png/170px-MOPC_60.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/MOPC_60.png/255px-MOPC_60.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/MOPC_60.png/340px-MOPC_60.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MOPC_60.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dancing at Mother of Peace AIDs orphanage, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a></div></div></div>
<p>Play is essential to the cognitive, physical, social, and emotional well-being of children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ginsburg_2007_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ginsburg_2007-41">[41]</a></sup> It offers children opportunities for physical (running, jumping, climbing, etc.), intellectual (social skills, community norms, ethics and general knowledge) and emotional development (empathy, compassion, and friendships). Unstructured play encourages creativity and imagination. Playing and interacting with other children, as well as some adults, provides opportunities for friendships, social interactions, conflicts and resolutions. However, adults tend to (often mistakenly) assume that virtually all children's social activities can be understood as "play" and, furthermore, that children's play activities do not involve much skill or effort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is through play that children at a very early age engage and interact in the world around them. Play allows children to create and explore a world they can master, conquering their fears while practicing adult roles, sometimes in conjunction with other children or adult caregivers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ginsburg_2007_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ginsburg_2007-41">[41]</a></sup> Undirected play allows children to learn how to work in groups, to share, to negotiate, to resolve conflicts, and to learn self-advocacy skills. However, when play is controlled by adults, children acquiesce to adult rules and concerns and lose some of the benefits play offers them. This is especially true in developing creativity, leadership, and group skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ginsburg_2007_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ginsburg_2007-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ralph_Hedley_The_tournament_1898.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3998" data-file-width="5103" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Ralph_Hedley_The_tournament_1898.jpg/220px-Ralph_Hedley_The_tournament_1898.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Ralph_Hedley_The_tournament_1898.jpg/330px-Ralph_Hedley_The_tournament_1898.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Ralph_Hedley_The_tournament_1898.jpg/440px-Ralph_Hedley_The_tournament_1898.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ralph_Hedley_The_tournament_1898.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ralph_Hedley" title="Ralph Hedley">Ralph Hedley</a>, <i>The Tournament,</i> 1898. It depicts poorer boys playing outdoors in a rural part of the <a href="/wiki/North_East_England" title="North East England">Northeast of England</a></div></div></div>
<p>Play is considered to be so important to optimal child development that it has been recognized by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Commission_on_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Commission on Human Rights">United Nations Commission on Human Rights</a> as a right of every child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-un_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-un-6">[6]</a></sup>Children who are being raised in a hurried and pressured style may limit the protective benefits they would gain from child-driven play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ginsburg_2007_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ginsburg_2007-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The initiation of play in a classroom setting allows teachers and students to interact through playfulness associated with a learning experience. Therefore, playfulness aids the interactions between adults and children in a learning environment. “Playful Structure” means to combine informal learning with formal learning to produce an effective learning experience for children at a young age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even though play is considered to be the most important to optimal child development, the environment affects their play and therefore their development. Poor children confront widespread environmental inequities as they experience less social support, and their parents are less responsive and more authoritarian. Children from low income families are less likely to have access to books and computers which would enhance their development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid14992634_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid14992634-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Street_culture">Street culture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_street_culture" title="Children's street culture">Children's street culture</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Children%27s_street_games" title="Children's street games">Children's street games</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boys_in_front_of_the_movie_theatre_on_Dundas_Street.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="718" decoding="async" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Boys_in_front_of_the_movie_theatre_on_Dundas_Street.jpg/220px-Boys_in_front_of_the_movie_theatre_on_Dundas_Street.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Boys_in_front_of_the_movie_theatre_on_Dundas_Street.jpg/330px-Boys_in_front_of_the_movie_theatre_on_Dundas_Street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Boys_in_front_of_the_movie_theatre_on_Dundas_Street.jpg/440px-Boys_in_front_of_the_movie_theatre_on_Dundas_Street.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boys_in_front_of_the_movie_theatre_on_Dundas_Street.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children in front of a movie theatre, Toronto, 1920s.</div></div></div>
<p>Children's street culture refers to the cumulative <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> created by young children and is sometimes referred to as their <i>secret world</i>. It is most common in children between the ages of seven and twelve. It is strongest in urban <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working class</a> <a href="/wiki/Industrial_district" title="Industrial district">industrial districts</a> where children are traditionally free to play out in the streets for long periods without supervision. It is invented and largely sustained by children themselves with little adult interference.
</p><p>Young children's street culture usually takes place on quiet backstreets and pavements, and along routes that venture out into local <a href="/wiki/Park" title="Park">parks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Playground" title="Playground">playgrounds</a>, scrub and wasteland, and to local shops. It often imposes imaginative status on certain sections of the urban realm (local buildings, kerbs, street objects, etc.). Children designate specific areas that serve as informal meeting and relaxation places (see: Sobel, 2001). An urban area that looks faceless or neglected to an adult may have deep '<a href="/wiki/Spirit_of_place" title="Spirit of place">spirit of place</a>' meanings in to children. Since the advent of indoor distractions such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_games" title="Video games">video games</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Television" title="Television">television</a>, concerns have been expressed about the vitality – or even the survival – of children's street culture.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographies_of_childhood">Geographies of childhood</span></h2>
<p>The geographies of childhood involves how (adult) society perceives the idea of childhood, the many ways adult attitudes and behaviors affect children's lives, including the environment which surrounds children and its implications.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The geographies of childhood is similar in some respects to <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_geographies" title="Children's geographies">children's geographies</a> which examines the places and spaces in which children live.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_deficit_disorder">Nature deficit disorder</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nature_deficit_disorder" title="Nature deficit disorder">Nature deficit disorder</a></div>
<p>Nature Deficit Disorder, a term coined by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Louv" title="Richard Louv">Richard Louv</a> in his 2005 book <i><a href="/wiki/Last_Child_in_the_Woods" title="Last Child in the Woods">Last Child in the Woods</a>,</i> refers to the trend in the United States and Canada towards less time for outdoor play,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> resulting in a wide range of behavioral problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>With increasing use of cellphones, computers, video games and television, children have more reasons to stay inside rather than outdoors exploring. “The average American child spends 44 hours a week with electronic media”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agitators_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agitators-52">[52]</a></sup> Research in 2007 has drawn a correlation between the declining number of <a href="/wiki/List_of_areas_in_the_United_States_National_Park_System" title="List of areas in the United States National Park System">National Park</a> visits in the U.S. and increasing consumption of electronic media by children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conservation_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conservation-53">[53]</a></sup> The media has accelerated the trend for children's nature disconnection by deemphasizing views of nature, as in Disney films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_responsibility">Age of responsibility</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Age_of_consent" title="Age of consent">Age of consent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_majority" title="Age of majority">Age of majority</a>, <a href="/wiki/Age_of_criminal_responsibility" title="Age of criminal responsibility">Age of criminal responsibility</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Marriageable_age" title="Marriageable age">Marriageable age</a></div>
<p>The age at which children are considered responsible for their society-bound actions (e. g. marriage, voting, etc.) has also changed over time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and this is reflected in the way they are treated in courts of law. In Roman times, children were regarded as not culpable for crimes, a position later adopted by the Church. In the 19th century, children younger than seven years old were believed incapable of crime. Children from the age of seven forward were considered responsible for their actions. Therefore, they could face criminal charges, be sent to adult prison, and be punished like adults by whipping, branding or hanging. However, courts at the time would consider the offender's age when deliberating sentencing.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Minimum employment age and marriage age also vary. The age limit of voluntary/involuntary military service is also disputed at the international level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuito_class.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Kuito_class.jpg/220px-Kuito_class.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Kuito_class.jpg/330px-Kuito_class.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Kuito_class.jpg/440px-Kuito_class.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kuito_class.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children in an outdoor classroom in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bi%C3%A9_(province)" title="Bié (province)">Bié</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="554" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg/220px-Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg/330px-Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg/440px-Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Torvisen_kansakoulu_1924-26.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Children seated in a Finnish classroom at the school of Torvinen in <a href="/wiki/Sodankyl%C3%A4" title="Sodankylä">Sodankylä</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, in the 1920s</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Education" title="Education">Education</a></div>
<p>Education, in the general sense, refers to the act or process of imparting or acquiring general knowledge, developing the powers of reasoning and judgment, and preparing intellectually for mature life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Formal education most often takes place through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schooling" title="Schooling">schooling</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Right_to_education" title="Right to education">right to education</a> has been recognized by some governments. At the global level, Article 13 of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>' 1966 <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Economic,_Social_and_Cultural_Rights" title="International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights">International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights</a> (ICESCR} recognizes the right of everyone to an education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICESCR-art13.1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICESCR-art13.1-58">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Compulsory_education" title="Compulsory education">Education is compulsory</a> in most places up to a certain age, but attendance at <a href="/wiki/School" title="School">school</a> may not be, with alternative options such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Home-schooling" title="Home-schooling">home-schooling</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-learning" title="E-learning">e-learning</a> being recognized as valid forms of education in certain jurisdictions.
</p><p>Children in some countries (especially in parts of Africa and Asia) are often kept out of school, or attend only for short periods. Data from <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> indicate that in 2011, 57 million children were out of school; and more than 20% of African children have never attended primary school or have left without completing primary education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> According to a UN report, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warfare" title="Warfare">warfare</a> is preventing 28 million children worldwide from receiving an education, due to the risk of sexual violence and attacks in schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Other factors that keep children out of school include poverty, child labor, social attitudes, and long distances to school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Attitudes_toward_children">Attitudes toward children</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Group_of_Breaker_boys._Smallest_is_Sam_Belloma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3276" data-file-width="4806" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Group_of_Breaker_boys._Smallest_is_Sam_Belloma.jpg/220px-Group_of_Breaker_boys._Smallest_is_Sam_Belloma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Group_of_Breaker_boys._Smallest_is_Sam_Belloma.jpg/330px-Group_of_Breaker_boys._Smallest_is_Sam_Belloma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Group_of_Breaker_boys._Smallest_is_Sam_Belloma.jpg/440px-Group_of_Breaker_boys._Smallest_is_Sam_Belloma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Group_of_Breaker_boys._Smallest_is_Sam_Belloma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Group of <a href="/wiki/Breaker_boy" title="Breaker boy">breaker boys</a> in Pittston, Pennsylvania, 1911. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_labor" title="Child labor">Child labor</a> was widespread until the early 20th century. In the 21st century, child labor rates are highest in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>.</div></div></div><p> Social attitudes toward children differ around the world in various cultures and change over time. A 1988 study on European attitudes toward the centrality of children found that Italy was more child-centric and the Netherlands less child-centric, with other countries, such as Austria, Great Britain, Ireland and <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> falling in between.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_marriage">Child marriage</span></h3>
<p>In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">child marriage</a> rates of female children under the age of 18 reached 75% in Niger, 68% in Central African Republic and Chad, 66% in Bangladesh, and 47% in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> According to a 2019 <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> report on child marriage, 37% of females were married before the age of 18 in sub-Saharan Africa, followed by South Asia at 30%. Lower levels were found in Latin America and Caribbean (25%), the Middle East and North Africa (18%), and Eastern Europe and Central Asia (11%), while rates in Western Europe and North America were minimal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Child marriage is more prevalent with girls, but also involves boys. A 2018 study in the journal Vulnerable Children and Youth Studies found that, worldwide, 4.5% of males are married before age 18, with the Central African Republic having the highest average rate at 27.9%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertility_and_number_of_children_per_woman">Fertility and number of children per woman</span></h3>
<p>Before <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">contraception</a> became widely available in the 20th century, women had little choice other than <a href="/wiki/Abstinence" title="Abstinence">abstinence</a> or having often many children. In fact, current <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> concerns have only become possible with drastically reduced <a href="/wiki/Child_mortality" title="Child mortality">child mortality</a> and sustained fertility. In 2017 the global <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rate</a> was estimated to be 2.37 children per woman,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup> adding about 80 million people to the world population per year. In order to measure the total number of children, scientists often prefer the completed cohort fertility at age 50 years (CCF50).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup> Although the number of children is also influenced by <a href="/wiki/Social_norm" title="Social norm">cultural norms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peer_pressure" title="Peer pressure">peer pressure</a> and other social factors, the CCF50 appears to be most heavily dependent on the educational level of women, ranging from 5-8 children in women without education to less than 2 in women with 12 or more years of education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Issues">Issues</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergencies_and_conflicts">Emergencies and conflicts</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Protection_of_Women_and_Children_in_Emergency_and_Armed_Conflict" title="Declaration on the Protection of Women and Children in Emergency and Armed Conflict">Declaration on the Protection of Women and Children in Emergency and Armed Conflict</a>, <a href="/wiki/Children_in_the_Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Children in the Israeli–Palestinian conflict">Children in the Israeli–Palestinian conflict</a>, <a href="/wiki/Save_the_Children" title="Save the Children">Save the Children</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inter-Agency_Network_for_Education_in_Emergencies" title="Inter-Agency Network for Education in Emergencies">Inter-Agency Network for Education in Emergencies</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_use_of_children" title="Military use of children">Military use of children</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trafficking_of_children" title="Trafficking of children">Trafficking of children</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_child_abduction" title="International child abduction">International child abduction</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Refugee_children" title="Refugee children">Refugee children</a></div>
<p>Emergencies and conflicts pose detrimental risks to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_Health" title="Child Health">health</a>, safety, and well-being of children. There are many different kinds of conflicts and emergencies, e.g. <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">wars</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_disaster" title="Natural disaster">natural disasters</a>. As of 2010 approximately 13 million children are displaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armed_conflicts" title="Armed conflicts">armed conflicts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Violence" title="Violence">violence</a> around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNICEF_2010_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNICEF_2010-68">[68]</a></sup> Where violent conflicts are the norm, the lives of young children are significantly disrupted and their families have great difficulty in offering the sensitive and consistent care that young children need for their healthy development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNICEF_2010_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNICEF_2010-68">[68]</a></sup> Studies on the effect of emergencies and conflict on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_health" title="Physical health">physical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> of children between birth and 8 years old show that where the disaster is natural, the rate of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">PTSD</a> occurs in anywhere from 3 to 87 percent of affected children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shannon_1994_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shannon_1994-69">[69]</a></sup> However, rates of PTSD for children living in chronic conflict conditions varies from 15 to 50 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_protection_2">Child protection</span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Child_protection" title="Child protection">Child protection</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Child_protection&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar nomobile nowraplinks hlist nowraplinks"><tbody><tr><th class="sidebar-title" style="background:#ccccff; border-bottom:2px solid #fafafa;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Childcare" title="Childcare">Childcare</a></th></tr><tr><th class="sidebar-heading" style="background:#ddddff;">

<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Child_Protection_and_Developement_Center_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="579" data-file-width="425" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Child_Protection_and_Developement_Center_2.jpg/220px-Child_Protection_and_Developement_Center_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Child_Protection_and_Developement_Center_2.jpg/330px-Child_Protection_and_Developement_Center_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/ff/Child_Protection_and_Developement_Center_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Child_Protection_and_Developement_Center_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A child protection and development center</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Child_protection" title="Child protection">Child protection</a> is the safeguarding of children from violence, exploitation, abuse, and neglect. Article 19 of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN_Convention_on_the_Rights_of_the_Child" title="UN Convention on the Rights of the Child">UN Convention on the Rights of the Child</a> provides for the protection of children in and out of the home. One of the ways to ensure this is by giving them <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_4" title="Sustainable Development Goal 4">quality education</a>, the fourth of the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a>, in addition to other child protection systems.
</p>
Child protection systems are a set of usually government-run services designed to protect children and young people who are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Underage" title="Underage">underage</a> and to encourage family stability. <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> defines<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> a ‘child protection system’ as: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>the set of laws, policies, regulations and services needed across all social sectors – especially social welfare, education, health, security and justice – to support prevention and response to protection-related risks. These systems are part of <a href="/wiki/Social_protection" title="Social protection">social protection</a>, and extend beyond it. At the level of prevention, their aim includes supporting and strengthening families to reduce social exclusion, and to lower the risk of separation, violence and exploitation. Responsibilities are often spread across government agencies, with services delivered by local authorities, non-State providers, and community groups, making coordination between sectors and levels, including routine referral systems etc.., a necessary component of effective child protection systems.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>United Nations Economic and Social Council (2008), UNICEF Child Protection Strategy, E/ICEF/2008/5/Rev.1, par. 12-13.</cite></div></blockquote></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_abuse_and_child_labor">Child abuse and child labor</span></h3>
<p>Protection of children from <a href="/wiki/Child_abuse" title="Child abuse">abuse</a> is considered an important contemporary goal. This includes protecting children from exploitation such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_labor" title="Child labor">child labor</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Child_trafficking" title="Child trafficking">child trafficking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_selling" title="Child selling">child selling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Child_sexual_abuse" title="Child sexual abuse">child sexual abuse</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Child_prostitution" title="Child prostitution">child prostitution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Child_pornography" title="Child pornography">child pornography</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_use_of_children" title="Military use of children">military use of children</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Child_laundering" title="Child laundering">child laundering</a> in illegal <a href="/wiki/Adoption" title="Adoption">adoptions</a>. There exist several international instruments for these purposes, such as:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Worst_Forms_of_Child_Labour_Convention" title="Worst Forms of Child Labour Convention">Worst Forms of Child Labour Convention</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Minimum_Age_Convention,_1973" title="Minimum Age Convention, 1973">Minimum Age Convention, 1973</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Optional_Protocol_on_the_Sale_of_Children,_Child_Prostitution_and_Child_Pornography" title="Optional Protocol on the Sale of Children, Child Prostitution and Child Pornography">Optional Protocol on the Sale of Children, Child Prostitution and Child Pornography</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe_Convention_on_the_Protection_of_Children_against_Sexual_Exploitation_and_Sexual_Abuse" title="Council of Europe Convention on the Protection of Children against Sexual Exploitation and Sexual Abuse">Council of Europe Convention on the Protection of Children against Sexual Exploitation and Sexual Abuse</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Optional_Protocol_on_the_Involvement_of_Children_in_Armed_Conflict" title="Optional Protocol on the Involvement of Children in Armed Conflict">Optional Protocol on the Involvement of Children in Armed Conflict</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hague_Adoption_Convention" title="Hague Adoption Convention">Hague Adoption Convention</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_and_children" title="Climate change and children">Climate change and children</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Climate_change_and_children&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p>Children are more vulnerable to the <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change" title="Effects of climate change">effects of climate change</a> than adults. <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">The World Health Organization</a> estimated that 88% of the existing global burden of disease is linked to climate change affecting children under 5 years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The Lancet review on health and climate change lists children as the worst-affected category by climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Children are also 14–44 percent more likely to die from environmental factors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> again leaving them the most vulnerable. Those in urban areas will be affected by lower air quality and overcrowding, and will struggle the most to better their situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Climate_change_and_children_who.int_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Climate_change_and_children_who.int-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Climate_Emergency_-_Child_Demo_415.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2070" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Climate_Emergency_-_Child_Demo_415.jpg/220px-Climate_Emergency_-_Child_Demo_415.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Climate_Emergency_-_Child_Demo_415.jpg/330px-Climate_Emergency_-_Child_Demo_415.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Climate_Emergency_-_Child_Demo_415.jpg/440px-Climate_Emergency_-_Child_Demo_415.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Climate_Emergency_-_Child_Demo_415.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Climate Emergency -</div></div></div>
<p>Children are physically more <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_vulnerability" title="Climate change vulnerability">vulnerable to climate change</a> in all its forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Climate_change_and_children_:2_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Climate_change_and_children_:2-77">[77]</a></sup> Climate change <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_climate_change_on_human_health" title="Effects of climate change on human health">affects the physical health</a> of a child and their well-being. Prevailing <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">inequalities</a>, between and within countries, determines how climate change impacts children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Children have no voice or attention in terms of global responses to climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Climate_change_and_children_:2_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Climate_change_and_children_:2-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
People living in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-income_countries" title="Low-income countries">low-income countries</a> suffer from a higher burden of disease and are less capable of facing climate change threats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Nearly every child in the world is at risk from climate change and pollution. Almost half are at extreme risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_mortality">Child mortality</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Child_mortality" title="Child mortality">Child mortality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">Infant mortality</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births,_under-5,_world_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="863" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg/360px-2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg/540px-2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg/720px-2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births%2C_under-5%2C_world_map.svg.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2012_Infant_mortality_rate_per_1000_live_births,_under-5,_world_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World infant mortality rates in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicef2013_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-unicef2013-81">[81]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>During the early 17th century in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, about two-thirds of all children died before the age of four.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the life expectancy of children increased dramatically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> This has continued in England, and in the 21st century child mortality rates have fallen across the world. About 12.6 million under-five infants died worldwide in 1990, which declined to 6.6 million in 2012. The infant mortality rate dropped from 90 deaths per 1,000 live births in 1990, to 48 in 2012. The highest average infant mortality rates are in sub-Saharan Africa, at 98 deaths per 1,000 live births – over double the world's average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unicef2013_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-unicef2013-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p>
